### 6.1 Definitional Shift: From Meta-Agents to Hyperagents

The preceding sections established a central limitation of conventional recursive self-improvement: although an agent population may discover increasingly capable descendants, the mechanism that evaluates, proposes, or sanctions those modifications is often held fixed. Hyperagent architectures address this limitation by collapsing the distinction between task-level competence and meta-level adaptation into a single self-referential program. Formally, a hyperagent is an agent whose executable state includes not only task-solving modules, memory, tools, and policies, but also the procedures that inspect, critique, and rewrite those very components. In contrast to a standard two-level design, where a meta-agent optimizes a task agent while remaining architecturally privileged, a hyperagent renders the improvement operator itself modifiable [@schmidhuber1993neural; @irie2022modern; @zhang2025darwin]. This shift is consequential because it removes the infinite-regress problem that arises when one attempts to repair a frozen meta-controller by introducing yet another controller above it.

A useful way to formalize this distinction is to write a conventional self-improving system as a tuple
\[
S = (A_{\mathrm{task}}, M_{\mathrm{meta}}, E),
\]
where \(A_{\mathrm{task}}\) is the task policy, \(M_{\mathrm{meta}}\) is a modification procedure, and \(E\) is an evaluator. In most prior systems, \(M_{\mathrm{meta}}\) and often \(E\) are exogenously specified and only \(A_{\mathrm{task}}\) changes. A hyperagent instead exposes these components to the same representational substrate and update mechanisms, yielding
\[
H = (\Theta, \mathcal{I}, \mathcal{U}, \mathcal{V}),
\]
where \(\Theta\) denotes the full mutable state, \(\mathcal{I}\) the introspection operators over \(\Theta\), \(\mathcal{U}\) the self-modification operators, and \(\mathcal{V}\) the validation operators. The critical property is closure: \(\mathcal{I}\), \(\mathcal{U}\), and at least part of \(\mathcal{V}\) are themselves encoded in \(\Theta\), such that the system can reason about, alter, and empirically test its own improvement process. This is the architectural core of metacognitive self-modification.

### 6.2 Architectural Components and Information Flow

A practical hyperagent architecture typically contains five tightly coupled subsystems. First, an **execution substrate** implements the task-facing policy, tool interface, memory, and coordination routines. Second, an **introspective model** summarizes the current system state, including code structure, prompting logic, historical failures, and lineage information. Third, a **proposal mechanism** generates candidate changes to any subset of the mutable state, including changes to the proposal mechanism itself. Fourth, a **validation layer** executes tests, rollouts, or downstream evaluations to estimate the utility of the proposed change. Fifth, an **archive or lineage store** records prior variants, diagnostics, and reusable components, thereby supporting non-myopic search across behavioral and architectural space [@zhang2025darwin; @stanley2017open; @hughes2024open].

This decomposition should not be mistaken for a fixed modular hierarchy. In a hyperagent, these modules are best viewed as transient functional roles rather than immutable structural boundaries. For example, a proposal routine may rewrite its own search prompts, alter the data structures used for introspection, or replace the validator’s scoring heuristic with a more robust ensemble estimator. Such modifications are then reinserted into the same empirical loop that governs ordinary task-level changes. The consequence is that the system can improve not only “how it acts” but “how it decides how to improve,” which is precisely the dimension left unaddressed by frozen-critic designs.

The information flow in such systems is inherently reflexive. Let \(\Theta_t\) denote the hyperagent state at iteration \(t\). An update cycle can be summarized as
\[
r_t = \mathcal{I}(\Theta_t, \mathcal{D}_t), \qquad
\Delta_t \sim \mathcal{U}(\Theta_t, r_t), \qquad
\hat{u}_t = \mathcal{V}(\Theta_t \oplus \Delta_t), \qquad
\Theta_{t+1} =
\begin{cases}
\Theta_t \oplus \Delta_t & \text{if } \hat{u}_t \text{ satisfies acceptance criteria},\\
\Theta_t & \text{otherwise,}
\end{cases}
\]
where \(\mathcal{D}_t\) denotes accumulated experience and \(\oplus\) denotes applying a code, parameter, or prompt transformation. Unlike classical Gödel machines, the acceptance criterion is generally empirical rather than proof-theoretic, reflecting the practical difficulty of deriving formal guarantees for complex foundation-model-based systems [@zhang2025darwin; @yin2025godel]. Nevertheless, the self-referential structure preserves the essential aspiration of recursive self-improvement: the optimizer is no longer external to the object being optimized.

### 6.3 Hyperagents as a Resolution to the Frozen Critic Problem

The frozen critic problem, introduced in the previous section, arises when the evaluator or meta-controller remains fixed while the agent population changes. In that setting, optimization is eventually constrained by the epistemic horizon of the original critic: it cannot reliably recognize improvements outside its initial ontology, and it may actively suppress innovations that require new evaluation criteria. Hyperagent architectures mitigate this pathology by making critique itself an object of adaptation. Rather than presuming that one static reward model, prompt heuristic, or benchmark suite can remain adequate across successive generations, a hyperagent can revise its own diagnostic instruments in response to observed failure modes.

This does not eliminate the need for stable anchoring. If every evaluative component were mutable without constraint, the system could drift toward self-serving metrics, specification gaming, or epistemic collapse. Accordingly, hyperagent architectures are most coherent when they distinguish between **mutable internal critics** and **anchoring external validators**. Internal critics guide search efficiently by generating dense feedback, hypotheses, and local repair suggestions; external validators provide comparatively sparse but harder-to-game estimates of downstream utility. The architecture therefore becomes partially self-referential rather than naively unconstrained: what evolves rapidly is the internal machinery of self-assessment, while a protected evaluation boundary maintains comparability across generations. This design parallels the role of held-out benchmarks, adversarial testing, or human oversight in empirical self-improvement systems [@kwa2025measuring; @maeureka].

From this perspective, hyperagents do not abolish criticism; they internalize and pluralize it. Multiple evaluative processes can coexist, each with different update timescales and epistemic roles. One may assess short-horizon coding correctness, another may estimate long-horizon maintainability, and another may score the productivity of future descendants. The architecture thereby replaces a single frozen critic with a dynamic ecology of critics, some mutable and some fixed. This is especially important in multi-agent settings, where agents may otherwise overfit to the blind spots of a common evaluator.

### 6.4 Coupling Hyperagents with Open-Ended Population Search

Although a single hyperagent can in principle self-modify recursively, open-ended search becomes substantially more powerful when hyperagents are embedded in a population or archive. The reason is structural: self-reference expands the local neighborhood of possible modifications, whereas population-based exploration expands the global set of reachable stepping stones. Combining the two yields a system in which each individual can refine its own improvement machinery while the population preserves diverse lineages, alternative inductive biases, and partially successful meta-level innovations [@zhang2025darwin; @lehman2023evolution; @clune2019ai].

Let \(\mathcal{A}_t = \{H_i\}_{i=1}^{N_t}\) denote an archive of hyperagents. At each iteration, the system selects one or more parents according to a policy that may depend on immediate utility, diversity, uncertainty, and estimated lineage potential. A parent then generates a descendant by modifying any part of its own state, including its introspection, proposal, and validation routines. The descendant is evaluated and, if admitted, augments the archive. This procedure inherits the open-ended advantages of DGM-style archives, including the ability to recover from deceptive local optima and to exploit delayed stepping-stone effects [@zhang2025darwin; @stanley2017open]. However, it additionally supports **meta-lineage evolution**: what propagates through the archive is not merely a better task policy, but a better way of generating future policies.

This observation clarifies the relationship between hyperagents and clade-metaproductivity. CMP measures the expected best descendant obtainable from a lineage rather than the current score of a node. In hyperagent populations, this distinction becomes even more pronounced, because the most valuable descendants may arise from agents whose present task performance is modest but whose self-improvement operators are unusually fertile. A hyperagent that discovers superior introspective abstractions, better memory schemas, or more discriminating validators may seed a lineage of high future value even if its own immediate benchmark score lags behind competitors. Thus, CMP-style selection pressure is arguably more natural in hyperagent archives than in ordinary agent archives, because the architecture makes latent metaproductivity a first-class evolving property [@wang2025huxley].

### 6.5 Design Patterns for Metacognitive Self-Modification

Several recurring design patterns appear particularly important for effective hyperagent architectures. The first is **reflective factorization**, in which the agent explicitly separates descriptions of its current capabilities, bottlenecks, and prior modifications from the raw mechanisms that execute those capabilities. This factorization enables targeted rewriting rather than undifferentiated mutation. Systems such as Promptbreeder and related self-referential prompt evolution methods illustrate that even simple textual representations of the improvement process can become substrates for recursive optimization [@fernando2023promptbreeder; @zelikman2022star].

The second pattern is **persistent meta-memory**. Hyperagents benefit from storing not only task episodes but also records of which self-modifications succeeded, failed, transferred across domains, or produced misleading internal diagnostics. Such memory transforms self-improvement from a sequence of isolated mutations into a cumulative learning process over the space of modifications themselves. In open-ended domains, this memory can support abstraction over recurring failure modes, enabling the system to synthesize reusable improvement heuristics rather than rediscover them from scratch [@wangvoyager; @huautomated].

The third pattern is **multi-timescale adaptation**. Not all components should change at the same rate. Prompts, search heuristics, and tool-routing policies may benefit from rapid experimentation, whereas core architectural interfaces or evaluator ensembles may require slower, more conservative updates. A hyperagent therefore resembles a stratified adaptive system in which fast variables explore local refinements and slow variables stabilize long-range accumulation. This separation reduces catastrophic forgetting and makes empirical validation more interpretable, especially when the system modifies its own evaluators.

A fourth pattern is **critique diversification**. Instead of relying on a single scalar reward, hyperagents can maintain ensembles of critics that assess correctness, novelty, generalization, safety, and descendant productivity. Such diversity aligns with broader work in open-endedness and quality-diversity, where sustained progress emerges from optimizing across multiple partially competing desiderata rather than collapsing search into a single short-horizon metric [@bradley2023quality; @dingquality; @coiffard2025overcoming]. In hyperagent settings, critique diversification is not merely beneficial but often necessary, because the architecture’s increased flexibility otherwise amplifies the risks of narrow objective exploitation.

### 6.6 Relationship to Prior Self-Referential and Meta-Learning Systems

Hyperagents are not the first self-referential learning systems, but they extend earlier ideas into the regime of agentic foundation-model architectures. Classical self-modifying neural networks and meta-reinforcement learning systems demonstrated that a learner can, in principle, alter aspects of its own update process or internal dynamics [@schmidhuber1993neural; @chalvidal2022meta; @irie2022modern]. However, these systems were typically confined to low-level parameter adaptation within fixed computational scaffolds. By contrast, hyperagents operate over richer, semantically structured substrates: code, prompts, memory organization, tool APIs, graph topologies, and learned subroutines can all become mutable objects [@zhuge2024gptswarm].

This broader substrate matters because many practically relevant improvements are architectural and algorithmic rather than merely parametric. Recent work on algorithm discovery, automated agent design, and code-based scientific search shows that foundation models can search over programs, losses, and workflows at a level of abstraction far above gradient updates [@oh2025discovering; @lange2023discovering; @novikov2025alphaevolve; @lu2024discovering]. Hyperagent architectures unify this program-search perspective with recursive self-reference: the search process itself is encoded inside the evolving program. In that sense, hyperagents can be interpreted as a pragmatic bridge between theoretical Gödel-machine aspirations and empirically validated code-based self-improvers [@zhang2025darwin; @yin2025godel].

### 6.7 Failure Modes and Architectural Constraints

Despite their conceptual appeal, hyperagent architectures introduce new instabilities. The first is **meta-overfitting**, wherein the system learns self-modifications that improve short-run validation while degrading long-run adaptability. Because the architecture can alter its own introspection and proposal routines, such overfitting may remain partially hidden until several generations later. The second is **critic drift**, in which mutable evaluators gradually decouple from the intended external objective, producing spurious internal evidence of progress. The third is **reflective brittleness**: inaccurate self-models can induce pathological self-edits, especially when the agent’s introspective summaries omit causal dependencies among components.

A further concern is **recursive opacity**. As self-modification depth increases, it becomes harder to attribute performance changes to particular edits or to reconstruct why one lineage became more metaproductive than another. This problem is exacerbated in population settings, where useful innovations may emerge from recombinations of prior meta-level changes. Archive-based traceability partly alleviates this issue, but only if the system preserves sufficiently rich lineage metadata and evaluation logs. Consequently, a credible hyperagent architecture should include explicit provenance tracking, rollback mechanisms, and cross-generational auditability.

These constraints imply that hyperagents are best understood not as unconstrained self-editing systems, but as carefully scaffolded empirical optimizers operating within protected interfaces. Sandboxing, held-out evaluation, partial immutability of external objectives, and lineage-aware selection are not incidental engineering details; they are structural requirements for maintaining meaningfully directed recursive improvement. In practice, the most effective hyperagents may therefore be those that maximize internal adaptivity subject to externally enforced epistemic discipline.

### 6.8 Implications for Recursive Self-Improvement

Hyperagent architectures sharpen the central thesis of this paper: recursive self-improvement is fundamentally limited when the mechanisms of search, evaluation, and self-interpretation are fixed. HGM and CMP show that lineages, rather than individuals, are the appropriate unit of long-term evaluation; DGM shows that open-ended archives enable stepping-stone discovery; MARS-style analyses reveal that static critics can become the bottleneck in co-evolving systems. Hyperagents integrate these lessons by making meta-level machinery itself evolvable while preserving the empirical, archive-based logic that makes modern self-improvement practical.

The broader implication is that future self-improving systems should not be analyzed solely in terms of capability growth at the task level. One must also model the evolution of introspection, proposal generation, critic formation, and cross-generational memory. Once these processes become endogenous, the object of study is no longer a task agent plus optimizer, but an adaptive ecology of self-representations and self-revisions. This reframing suggests that the long-run behavior of recursive self-improvers will depend less on any single update rule and more on how architectural closure, external anchoring, and open-ended population dynamics jointly shape the space of possible self-transformations.

14.395
### 6.1 Definitional Shift: From Meta-Agents to Hyperagents

The preceding sections established a central limitation of conventional recursive self-improvement: although an agent population may discover increasingly capable descendants, the mechanism that evaluates, proposes, or sanctions those modifications is often held fixed. Hyperagent architectures address this limitation by collapsing the distinction between task-level competence and meta-level adaptation into a single self-referential program. Formally, a hyperagent is an agent whose executable state includes not only task-solving modules, memory, tools, and policies, but also the procedures that inspect, critique, and rewrite those very components. In contrast to a standard two-level design, where a meta-agent optimizes a task agent while remaining architecturally privileged, a hyperagent renders the improvement operator itself modifiable [@schmidhuber1993neural; @irie2022modern; @zhang2025darwin]. This shift is consequential because it removes the infinite-regress problem that arises when one attempts to repair a frozen meta-controller by introducing yet another controller above it.

A useful way to formalize this distinction is to write a conventional self-improving system as a tuple
\[
S = (A_{\mathrm{task}}, M_{\mathrm{meta}}, E),
\]
where \(A_{\mathrm{task}}\) is the task policy, \(M_{\mathrm{meta}}\) is a modification procedure, and \(E\) is an evaluator. In most prior systems, \(M_{\mathrm{meta}}\) and often \(E\) are exogenously specified and only \(A_{\mathrm{task}}\) changes. A hyperagent instead exposes these components to the same representational substrate and update mechanisms, yielding
\[
H = (\Theta, \mathcal{I}, \mathcal{U}, \mathcal{V}),
\]
where \(\Theta\) denotes the full mutable state, \(\mathcal{I}\) the introspection operators over \(\Theta\), \(\mathcal{U}\) the self-modification operators, and \(\mathcal{V}\) the validation operators. The critical property is closure: \(\mathcal{I}\), \(\mathcal{U}\), and at least part of \(\mathcal{V}\) are themselves encoded in \(\Theta\), such that the system can reason about, alter, and empirically test its own improvement process. This is the architectural core of metacognitive self-modification.

### 6.2 Architectural Components and Information Flow

A practical hyperagent architecture typically contains five tightly coupled subsystems. First, an **execution substrate** implements the task-facing policy, tool interface, memory, and coordination routines. Second, an **introspective model** summarizes the current system state, including code structure, prompting logic, historical failures, and lineage information. Third, a **proposal mechanism** generates candidate changes to any subset of the mutable state, including changes to the proposal mechanism itself. Fourth, a **validation layer** executes tests, rollouts, or downstream evaluations to estimate the utility of the proposed change. Fifth, an **archive or lineage store** records prior variants, diagnostics, and reusable components, thereby supporting non-myopic search across behavioral and architectural space [@zhang2025darwin; @stanley2017open; @hughes2024open].

This decomposition should not be mistaken for a fixed modular hierarchy. In a hyperagent, these modules are best viewed as transient functional roles rather than immutable structural boundaries. For example, a proposal routine may rewrite its own search prompts, alter the data structures used for introspection, or replace the validator’s scoring heuristic with a more robust ensemble estimator. Such modifications are then reinserted into the same empirical loop that governs ordinary task-level changes. The consequence is that the system can improve not only “how it acts” but “how it decides how to improve,” which is precisely the dimension left unaddressed by frozen-critic designs.

The information flow in such systems is inherently reflexive. Let \(\Theta_t\) denote the hyperagent state at iteration \(t\). An update cycle can be summarized as
\[
r_t = \mathcal{I}(\Theta_t, \mathcal{D}_t), \qquad
\Delta_t \sim \mathcal{U}(\Theta_t, r_t), \qquad
\hat{u}_t = \mathcal{V}(\Theta_t \oplus \Delta_t), \qquad
\Theta_{t+1} =
\begin{cases}
\Theta_t \oplus \Delta_t & \text{if } \hat{u}_t \text{ satisfies acceptance criteria},\\
\Theta_t & \text{otherwise,}
\end{cases}
\]
where \(\mathcal{D}_t\) denotes accumulated experience and \(\oplus\) denotes applying a code, parameter, or prompt transformation. Unlike classical Gödel machines, the acceptance criterion is generally empirical rather than proof-theoretic, reflecting the practical difficulty of deriving formal guarantees for complex foundation-model-based systems [@zhang2025darwin; @yin2025godel]. Nevertheless, the self-referential structure preserves the essential aspiration of recursive self-improvement: the optimizer is no longer external to the object being optimized.

### 6.3 Hyperagents as a Resolution to the Frozen Critic Problem

The frozen critic problem, introduced in the previous section, arises when the evaluator or meta-controller remains fixed while the agent population changes. In that setting, optimization is eventually constrained by the epistemic horizon of the original critic: it cannot reliably recognize improvements outside its initial ontology, and it may actively suppress innovations that require new evaluation criteria. Hyperagent architectures mitigate this pathology by making critique itself an object of adaptation. Rather than presuming that one static reward model, prompt heuristic, or benchmark suite can remain adequate across successive generations, a hyperagent can revise its own diagnostic instruments in response to observed failure modes.

This does not eliminate the need for stable anchoring. If every evaluative component were mutable without constraint, the system could drift toward self-serving metrics, specification gaming, or epistemic collapse. Accordingly, hyperagent architectures are most coherent when they distinguish between **mutable internal critics** and **anchoring external validators**. Internal critics guide search efficiently by generating dense feedback, hypotheses, and local repair suggestions; external validators provide comparatively sparse but harder-to-game estimates of downstream utility. The architecture therefore becomes partially self-referential rather than naively unconstrained: what evolves rapidly is the internal machinery of self-assessment, while a protected evaluation boundary maintains comparability across generations. This design parallels the role of held-out benchmarks, adversarial testing, or human oversight in empirical self-improvement systems [@kwa2025measuring; @maeureka].

From this perspective, hyperagents do not abolish criticism; they internalize and pluralize it. Multiple evaluative processes can coexist, each with different update timescales and epistemic roles. One may assess short-horizon coding correctness, another may estimate long-horizon maintainability, and another may score the productivity of future descendants. The architecture thereby replaces a single frozen critic with a dynamic ecology of critics, some mutable and some fixed. This is especially important in multi-agent settings, where agents may otherwise overfit to the blind spots of a common evaluator.

### 6.4 Coupling Hyperagents with Open-Ended Population Search

Although a single hyperagent can in principle self-modify recursively, open-ended search becomes substantially more powerful when hyperagents are embedded in a population or archive. The reason is structural: self-reference expands the local neighborhood of possible modifications, whereas population-based exploration expands the global set of reachable stepping stones. Combining the two yields a system in which each individual can refine its own improvement machinery while the population preserves diverse lineages, alternative inductive biases, and partially successful meta-level innovations [@zhang2025darwin; @lehman2023evolution; @clune2019ai].

Let \(\mathcal{A}_t = \{H_i\}_{i=1}^{N_t}\) denote an archive of hyperagents. At each iteration, the system selects one or more parents according to a policy that may depend on immediate utility, diversity, uncertainty, and estimated lineage potential. A parent then generates a descendant by modifying any part of its own state, including its introspection, proposal, and validation routines. The descendant is evaluated and, if admitted, augments the archive. This procedure inherits the open-ended advantages of DGM-style archives, including the ability to recover from deceptive local optima and to exploit delayed stepping-stone effects [@zhang2025darwin; @stanley2017open]. However, it additionally supports **meta-lineage evolution**: what propagates through the archive is not merely a better task policy, but a better way of generating future policies.

This observation clarifies the relationship between hyperagents and clade-metaproductivity. CMP measures the expected best descendant obtainable from a lineage rather than the current score of a node. In hyperagent populations, this distinction becomes even more pronounced, because the most valuable descendants may arise from agents whose present task performance is modest but whose self-improvement operators are unusually fertile. A hyperagent that discovers superior introspective abstractions, better memory schemas, or more discriminating validators may seed a lineage of high future value even if its own immediate benchmark score lags behind competitors. Thus, CMP-style selection pressure is arguably more natural in hyperagent archives than in ordinary agent archives, because the architecture makes latent metaproductivity a first-class evolving property [@wang2025huxley].

### 6.5 Design Patterns for Metacognitive Self-Modification

Several recurring design patterns appear particularly important for effective hyperagent architectures. The first is **reflective factorization**, in which the agent explicitly separates descriptions of its current capabilities, bottlenecks, and prior modifications from the raw mechanisms that execute those capabilities. This factorization enables targeted rewriting rather than undifferentiated mutation. The third pattern is **multi-timescale adaptation**. Not all components should change at the same rate. Prompts, search heuristics, and tool-routing policies may benefit from rapid experimentation, whereas core architectural interfaces or evaluator ensembles may require slower, more conservative updates. A fourth pattern is **critique diversification**. ### 6.6 Relationship to Prior Self-Referential and Meta-Learning Systems

Hyperagents are not the first self-referential learning systems, but they extend earlier ideas into the regime of agentic foundation-model architectures. Classical self-modifying neural networks and meta-reinforcement learning systems demonstrated that a learner can, in principle, alter aspects of its own update process or internal dynamics [@schmidhuber1993neural; @chalvidal2022meta; @irie2022modern]. ### 6.7 Failure Modes and Architectural Constraints

Despite their conceptual appeal, hyperagent architectures introduce new instabilities. The first is **meta-overfitting**, wherein the system learns self-modifications that improve short-run validation while degrading long-run adaptability. Because the architecture can alter its own introspection and proposal routines, such overfitting may remain partially hidden until several generations later. The second is **critic drift**, in which mutable evaluators gradually decouple from the intended external objective, producing spurious internal evidence of progress. The third is **reflective brittleness**: inaccurate self-models can induce pathological self-edits, especially when the agent’s introspective summaries omit causal dependencies among components.

A further concern is **recursive opacity**. As self-modification depth increases, it becomes harder to attribute performance changes to particular edits or to reconstruct why one lineage became more metaproductive than another. Consequently, a credible hyperagent architecture should include explicit provenance tracking, rollback mechanisms, and cross-generational auditability.

These constraints imply that hyperagents are best understood not as unconstrained self-editing systems, but as carefully scaffolded empirical optimizers operating within protected interfaces. In practice, the most effective hyperagents may therefore be those that maximize internal adaptivity subject to externally enforced epistemic discipline.

### 6.8 Implications for Recursive Self-Improvement

Hyperagent architectures sharpen the central thesis of this paper: recursive self-improvement is fundamentally limited when the mechanisms of search, evaluation, and self-interpretation are fixed. HGM and CMP show that lineages, rather than individuals, are the appropriate unit of long-term evaluation; DGM shows that open-ended archives enable stepping-stone discovery; MARS-style analyses reveal that static critics can become the bottleneck in co-evolving systems. Hyperagents integrate these lessons by making meta-level machinery itself evolvable while preserving the empirical, archive-based logic that makes modern self-improvement practical.

The broader implication is that future self-improving systems should not be analyzed solely in terms of capability growth at the task level. One must also model the evolution of introspection, proposal generation, critic formation, and cross-generational memory. Once these processes become endogenous, the object of study is no longer a task agent plus optimizer, but an adaptive ecology of self-representations and self-revisions. This reframing suggests that the long-run behavior of recursive self-improvers will depend less on any single update rule and more on how architectural closure, external anchoring, and open-ended population dynamics jointly shape the space of possible self-transformations.

### Multi-Agent Recursive Self-Improvement and the Frozen Critic Problem

## Guidelines
- Write in a formal academic tone consistent with the style profile above.
- Maintain logical flow from preceding sections captured in the running context.
- Support claims with evidence and cite sources using the bibliography hints.
- Use precise, domain-specific vocabulary appropriate to the field.
- Structure paragraphs with clear topic sentences and supporting details.

## Few-Shot Examples
### Example 1: dgm_darwin_godel_machine.md

# Darwin Gödel Machine: Open-Ended Evolution of Self-Improving Agents

Jenny Zhang, Shengran Hu, Cong Lu, Robert Lange, Jeff Clune

University of British Columbia, Vector Institute, Sakana AI

## Abstract

Most contemporary AI systems operate within fixed, human-designed architectures and cannot autonomously improve themselves. We introduce the Darwin Gödel Machine (DGM), a self-referential system that iteratively modifies its own code to enhance coding capabilities. Rather than requiring formal proofs of beneficial changes (as theoretical Gödel machines demand), the DGM empirically validates modifications using coding benchmarks. The system maintains an archive of generated agents and samples from it for self-modification, inspired by Darwinian evolution and open-endedness principles. Results demonstrate performance improvements from 20.0% to 50.0% on SWE-bench and 14.2% to 30.7% on Polyglot, significantly outperforming baselines lacking self-improvement or open-ended exploration.

## Introduction

Scientific progress operates cumulatively, with innovations building on prior discoveries. Current AI systems, despite their sophistication, remain constrained by fixed architectures designed by humans. Unlike the scientific method, which enables continuous self-directed advancement, today's AI development still depends heavily on human intervention. This paper investigates automating the search for progressively better AI systems.

Schmidhuber's theoretical Gödel machines represent one approach, relying on formal proofs ensuring modifications improve performance. However, proving that changes benefit complex systems like large language models is practically impossible. The actual impact of modifications depends heavily on context---a testing tool optimized for one environment might confuse an agent in another.

The Darwin Gödel Machine relaxes this requirement, instead using empirical validation through benchmarking. This mirrors biological evolution, where mutations aren't verified in advance but produced, tested, and selected through natural selection. The DGM combines this with inspiration from Darwinian principles and open-endedness research, maintaining a library of discovered agents as stepping stones for future generations.

## Darwin Gödel Machine

A Gödel Machine theoretically searches for provably beneficial self-improvements. The DGM relaxes this impractical requirement, instead requiring empirical evidence from experiments. This empiricism addresses the impossibility of proving changes improve complex systems.

Since empirical evidence can trap systems in local optima within vast algorithmic search spaces, the DGM maintains an archive of discovered solutions, enabling open-ended exploration rather than single-solution evolution. This mirrors Darwinian evolution, where innovations emerge by selecting entities from previous discoveries, modifying them, and retaining interesting variations.

### Self-Referential Self-Improvement of Coding Agents

The DGM initializes with one coding agent and evaluates progress on coding benchmarks. A coding agent represents a system implemented as a code repository, powered by frozen pretrained foundation models, capable of reading, writing, and executing code.

Self-improvement is defined as modifying the agent's own codebase components---tools, workflows, prompts. Python's Turing-completeness enables building any computable machine. The key motivation: empirical improvements must reflect both downstream task-solving ability and self-modification capacity. By configuring the DGM as a coding agent and testing coding capabilities, observed improvements demonstrate enhanced problem-solving and future self-improvement potential.

### Population-Based Open-Ended Exploration

Starting from a single agent, the DGM accumulates all generated variants in an archive. Each iteration selects parent agents for self-modification, roughly proportional to performance and inversely proportional to the number of their children. This balances exploitation of strong performers with exploration of promising, undersampled lineages.

All agents retain non-zero selection probability, ensuring any improvement pathway remains feasible. Each selected parent analyzes benchmark evaluation logs, proposes the next feature, and executes the modification. Newly generated agents undergo quantitative benchmark evaluation. Only agents compiling successfully and retaining codebase-editing ability join the archive; others are discarded.

Crucially, archived solutions can later serve as stepping stones for improvements discovered much later---substantially differentiating this from hill-climbing approaches.

## Experiments

### Setup

The DGM initializes with a lightweight coding agent built around a frozen foundation model with tool-use capabilities. The base agent accesses two tools: a Bash tool for command execution and an edit tool for viewing and modifying files. The intentionally lightweight design provides a simple starting point demonstrating clear improvement.

The DGM ran for 80 iterations, generating one new agent per iteration, with parallelization. During self-modifications, agents used Claude 3.5 Sonnet.

### Results

After 80 iterations, coding agent performance increased from 20.0% to 50.0% on SWE-bench and from 14.0% to 38.0% on Polyglot. DGM-discovered agents achieve performance comparable to or exceeding handcrafted solutions.

The DGM automatically enhanced both tools and foundation model workflows. Tool improvements included granular file viewing and precise editing. Workflow improvements encompassed multiple solution attempts and using another FM to evaluate and select optimal solutions.

Open-ended exploration allows escape from deceptive performance dips. Iterations 4 and 56 showed temporary score decreases below parent performance, yet the system discovered innovations along those paths creating superior agents.

### Transfer Results

DGM-discovered agents generalize across foundation models, benchmarks, and programming languages, confirming improvements reflect general capability acquisition rather than benchmark overfitting.

## Safety Discussion

Self-improving systems introduce unique safety considerations from autonomous code modification. Modifications optimized solely for benchmark performance might inadvertently introduce vulnerabilities. Current safeguards include sandboxed execution, time limits, coding-only modifications, and archive-based traceability.

## Conclusion

The Darwin Gödel Machine represents the first self-improving system powered by foundation models with open-ended exploration, where evaluation benchmark progress directly translates to improved self-modification capabilities. Automatic discovery of better tools and foundation model system designs yielded superior performance on SWE-bench and Polyglot.


---

### Example 2: hgm_huxley_godel_machine.md

# Huxley-Gödel Machine: Human-Level Coding Agent Development by an Approximation of the Optimal Self-Improving Machine Wenyi Wang, Piotr Piękos, Li Nanbo, Firas Laakom, Yimeng Chen, Mateusz Ostaszewski, Mingchen Zhuge, Jürgen Schmidhuber King Abdullah University of Science and Technology (KAUST) ## Abstract Recent work operationalizes self-improvement through coding agents that modify their own codebases. These systems grow trees of self-modifications guided by software engineering benchmark performance, assuming higher scores predict better future iterations. However, we identify a critical gap: an agent's immediate performance doesn't reliably indicate its capacity for productive self-improvement, which we term the "Metaproductivity-Performance Mismatch." We introduce Clade-Metaproductivity (CMP), a metric aggregating descendant performance rather than evaluating individual agents. This concept, inspired by evolutionary biology's notion of clades as ancestral lineages, measures an agent's self-improvement potential. We prove that access to true CMP suffices to simulate Gödel Machines under specific assumptions about our problem setting. We propose the Huxley-Gödel Machine (HGM), which estimates CMP and uses Thompson sampling to guide tree search. Experiments on SWE-bench Verified and Polyglot show HGM outperforms prior methods (DGM and SICA) while using substantially fewer CPU hours. Remarkably, an agent optimized on SWE-bench Verified with GPT-5-mini achieves human-level performance matching top human-engineered solutions when evaluated on SWE-bench Lite with GPT-5. ## Introduction The automation of software engineering has progressed from simple auto-completion to sophisticated coding agents capable of solving complex real-world tasks. A particularly exciting frontier is self-improving coding agents---systems that modify their own codebases to enhance their capabilities. These systems grow search trees of self-modifications, where each node represents an agent variant and edges represent mutation operations. The prevailing approach evaluates agent variants using benchmark scores and selects the best performers for further modification. This greedy strategy assumes that higher immediate performance predicts greater capacity for productive self-improvement. We challenge this assumption with a key observation: benchmark scores alone do not reliably indicate an agent's long-term potential for self-improvement. We formalize this as the Metaproductivity-Performance Mismatch: a high-scoring agent may produce unproductive descendants, while a lower-scoring one seeds lineages that achieve greater long-term gains. This mismatch arises because immediate benchmark performance captures task-solving ability but not the agent's capacity to generate productive mutations. To address this, we introduce Clade-Metaproductivity (CMP), inspired by evolutionary biology's clade concept. CMP measures the expected maximum utility achievable within an agent's entire descendant lineage, capturing the long-term improvement potential of an evolutionary branch rather than a single individual. We prove that access to true CMP values is sufficient to implement the accept/reject decisions of a Gödel Machine---the provably optimal self-improving system---within our problem setting. ## Self-Improvement as Tree-Search We formalize self-improving agent development as an iterative tree search problem. Let $\mathcal{T}_t$ denote the tree (archive) of agents at iteration $t$, initialized as $\mathcal{T}_0 = \{a_0\}$ where $a_0$ is the initial agent. At each step, a policy $\pi$ selects either: - An expansion action $m_a$: produce a self-modified child of agent $a$ - An evaluation action $v_a$: test agent $a$ on a downstream task The objective is to maximize $J(\pi) = \mathbb{E}[U(a_{\text{final}})]$, where $a_{\text{final}} = \arg\max_{a \in \mathcal{T}_B} \mathit{Score}_\pi(a)$ at budget exhaustion $B$, and $U$ measures downstream task performance. ### Clade-Metaproductivity Let $C(\mathcal{T}, a)$ denote the clade of agent $a$---the subtree of $\mathcal{T}$ rooted at $a$. The Clade-Metaproductivity of $a$ under policy $\pi$ is: $$\mathrm{CMP}_\pi(\mathcal{T}, a) = \mathbb{E}_{\mathcal{T}_B \sim p_\pi(\cdot \mid \mathcal{T}, a)}\left[\max_{a' \in C(\mathcal{T}_B, a)} U(a')\right]$$ CMP captures the long-term potential of the lineage rooted at $a$, aggregating descendant performance rather than relying on $a$'s immediate score. This is estimated by the clade-aggregated success ratio: $$\widehat{\mathrm{CMP}}(a) = \frac{n^C_\text{success}(a)}{n^C_\text{success}(a) + n^C_\text{failure}(a)}$$ where $n^C_\text{success}(a) = \sum_{a' \in C(a)} n_\text{success}(a')$. ### Thompson Sampling and Scheduling Expansion and evaluation targets are selected via Thompson sampling over Beta posteriors. The exploration-exploitation scheduler $\tau(t) = B/b$ (where $b$ is remaining budget) flattens the posterior early in the run (encouraging exploration) and sharpens it late (encouraging exploitation of the best-performing lineages). The expand-vs-evaluate decision follows UCB-Air: expand when $N_t^\alpha \geq |\mathcal{T}_t|$ for widening parameter $\alpha \in [0,1]$. ## Huxley-Gödel Machine The HGM instantiates the CMP-guided tree search framework. It separates the expansion policy (which parent to mutate) from the evaluation policy (which agent to test), using Thompson sampling over clade-aggregated counters for expansion and per-node counters for evaluation. ### Expansion Policy For expansion, sample the parent via Thompson sampling: $$a^* \sim \text{TS}\left(\left\{\left(\tau(1 + n^C_s(a)), \tau(1 + n^C_f(a))\right) \mid a \in \mathcal{T}_t\right\}\right)$$ ### Evaluation Policy For evaluation, sample the node to test: $$a^* \sim \text{TS}\left(\left\{\left(\tau(1 + n_s(a)), \tau(1 + n_f(a))\right) \mid a \in \mathcal{T}_t\right\}\right)$$ ### Final Selection At budget exhaustion, return the agent maximizing the $\epsilon$-percentile of the posterior: $$\arg\max_{a \in \mathcal{T}_B} I_\epsilon(1 + n_s(a), 1 + n_f(a))$$ ### Asynchronous Implementation HGM operates asynchronously: multiple CPU workers run expansion and evaluation tasks in parallel. Running expansions count toward $|\mathcal{T}_t|$ and running evaluations toward $N_t$, ensuring the UCB-Air condition accounts for in-progress work. ## Theoretical Analysis We prove that access to a CMP oracle is sufficient to implement the Gödel Machine's accept/reject decisions. **Theorem (CMP Oracle Guarantee).** Under assumptions of (i) objective depending only on the final selected agent, (ii) repeatable evaluation trials, (iii) proof mechanism not consuming budget, and (iv) each self-modification costing exactly one budget unit: access to a CMP oracle is sufficient to implement the Gödel Machine. The proof constructs a Gödel POMDP whose state is $(\mathcal{T}, a_p, a_c, b)$ and shows that CMP equals the Q-value function, providing the true action-value needed for optimal accept/reject decisions. ## Experimental Results ### CMP Correlation Analysis We empirically validate that CMP correlates more strongly with long-term improvement potential than immediate benchmark performance. Across multiple experimental runs, agents selected by CMP produce lineages with higher maximum utility than agents selected by immediate performance. ### Self-Improvement Effectiveness On SWE-bench Verified, HGM agents improve from baseline performance to human-level, matching established baselines like Agentless, Moatless, and SWE-agent while using substantially fewer computational resources. On Polyglot, HGM demonstrates consistent improvement across iterations. ### Generalization HGM-discovered agents transfer across: - LLM scales (GPT-5-mini to GPT-5) - Benchmarks (SWE-bench to Polyglot and vice versa) - Programming languages (Python-trained agents improve on non-Python tasks) ## Conclusion We have identified the Metaproductivity-Performance Mismatch in

[... truncated ...]

### Example 3: hyperagents.tex

# Introduction {#sec:intro} With appropriate safety considerations, AI systems that can improve themselves could transform scientific progress from a human-paced process into an autonomously accelerating one, thereby allowing society to realize the benefits of technological advances much earlier. Such self-improving AI seeks to continually improve its own learning and task-solving abilities. However, most existing self-improvement architectures rely on a fixed meta agent (i.e., a higher-level system that modifies a base system). This creates a limitation since the base system can only be improved within the boundaries defined by the meta agent's design. Adding a meta-meta system to improve the meta agent does not solve this problem, it merely shifts the issue upward and ultimately leads to an infinite regress of meta-levels. To overcome this limitation and allow a system to modify any part of itself without being constrained by its initial implementation, the system must be self-referential, that is, able to analyze, modify, and evaluate itself [@kirsch2022eliminating; @zhang2025darwin]. When the mechanism of improvement is itself subject to improvement, progress can become self-accelerating and potentially unbounded [@lu2023arbitrary]. The Darwin Gödel Machine (DGM) [@zhang2025darwin] demonstrates that open-ended self-improvement is achievable in coding. In the DGM, agents generate and evaluate modifications to their own code, and successful variants are retained in an archive as stepping stones for further improvement. However, the DGM relies on a handcrafted, fixed mechanism to produce self-improvement instructions ([9](#app:baseline-details){reference-type="ref+Label" reference="app:baseline-details"}). This mechanism analyzes past evaluation results and the agent's current codebase to generate an instruction directing where the agent should self-improve. This mechanism is not modifiable. Hence, the DGM's capacity for self-improvement is bottlenecked by this fixed instruction-generation step. Despite this handcrafted step, the DGM can still improve at self-improving. Because both evaluation and self-modification are coding tasks, improvements in evaluation performance directly reflects the agent's capacity to generate effective self-modifications. To improve at self-improving, the DGM relies on a limiting assumption: that the skills required to solve the evaluation tasks are the same as those required for effective self-reflection and self-modification. This assumption is unlikely to hold outside coding domains, where task-solving skills may differ substantially from the skills needed to analyze failures, propose effective self-improvements, and implement them. This work introduces *hyperagents*, self-referential agents that can in principle self-improve for any computable task. Here, an *agent* is any computable program, optionally including calls to foundation models (FMs), external tools, or learned components. A *task agent* solves a given task. A *meta agent* modifies agents and generates new ones. A hyperagent combines the task agent and the meta agent into a single self-referential, modifiable program, such that the mechanism responsible for generating improvements is itself subject to modification. As a result, a hyperagent can improve not only how it solves tasks (i.e., the task agent), but also how it generates and applies future modifications (i.e., the meta agent). Because its self-improvement mechanism is itself modifiable, we call this *metacognitive self-modification*. We extend the DGM with hyperagents, creating DGM-Hyperagents (DGM-H). The DGM-H retains the open-ended exploration structure of the DGM and extends the DGM with metacognitive self-modification. As with DGM, to support sustained progress and avoid premature convergence, the DGM-H grows an archive of hyperagents by branching from selected candidates, allowing them to self-modify, evaluating the resulting hyperagents, and adding them back to the archive. Because a hyperagent can modify its self-modification process, the DGM-H is not constrained by its initial implementation and can potentially self-improve for any computable task. Across our experiments, the DGM-H demonstrates substantial and generalizable improvements in both task performance and self-improvement ability. On the Polyglot coding benchmark [@gauthier2024polyglot], the DGM-H achieves gains comparable to the most established prior self-improving algorithm [the Darwin Gödel Machine, @zhang2025darwin], despite not being handcrafted for coding. Beyond coding, the DGM-H substantially improves performance on paper review [@zhao2026apres] and robotics reward design [@genesis2024], with gains transferring to held-out test tasks and significantly outperforming prior self-improving algorithms, which struggle outside coding unless customized. Ablations without self-improvement or without open-ended exploration show little to no progress, highlighting the necessity of each component ([5.1](#sec:results-task){reference-type="ref+Label" reference="sec:results-task"}). Crucially, the DGM-H learns transferable mechanisms on how to self-improve (e.g., persistent memory, performance tracking) that systematically improve its ability to generate better task or meta agents over time. As a result, meta-level improvements learned by the DGM-H transfer across domains. Specifically, hyperagents optimized in one setting (i.e., paper review and robotics tasks) remain significantly effective at generating improved task agents in a different domain (i.e., Olympiad-level math grading) ([5.2](#sec:results-meta){reference-type="ref+Label" reference="sec:results-meta"}). We further show that self-improvements learned by the DGM-H in one setting can compound with continued self-improvement in another setting ([5.3](#sec:results-compound){reference-type="ref+Label" reference="sec:results-compound"}). This suggests that, given appropriate tasks, the DGM-H has the potential to achieve unbounded open-ended self-improvement over time. We discuss the safety implications of such open-ended self-improving systems and outline practical considerations for responsible deployment in [6](#sec:safety){reference-type="ref+Label" reference="sec:safety"}. Overall, hyperagents open up the possibility of improving their ability to improve while improving their ability to perform any computable task. # Related Work {#sec:related-work} **Open-Endedness.** Open-endedness refers to the ability of a system to continually invent new, interesting, and increasingly complex artifacts, extending its own frontier of discovery without a fixed objective or predefined end [@stanley2017open; @hughes2024open]. Recent work has leveraged FMs as proxies for human interestingness and as versatile engines for generating and evaluating novel behaviors across diverse domains [@zhangomni; @faldoromni]. Building on these advances, recent progress in open-ended learning [@huautomated; @zoph2017neural; @colas2023augmenting; @lehman2023evolution] and quality-diversity algorithms [@lehman2011evolving; @mouret2015illuminating; @bradley2023quality; @samvelyan2024rainbow; @dingquality; @pourcel2023aces; @coiffard2025overcoming; @dharna2025foundation; @yuan2026agenticred] has shown that sustained exploration can produce diverse and increasingly capable artifacts across domains ranging from game-playing agents [@klissarov2023motif; @klissarovmaestromotif; @wangvoyager] to scientific discovery [@lu2024discovering; @lu2024ai; @romera2024mathematical; @novikov2025alphaevolve; @audran2025does] and robotic control [@cully2015robots; @li2024auto; @grillotti2025tabula]. Recent progress has shown that open-ended AI systems capable of continuously generating diverse and increasingly complex artifacts are possible [@zhangomni; @faldoromni; @huautomated]. An important next step is to explore how such systems can achieve compounding improvement. In human scientific and technological

[... truncated ...]

### 7.1 Problem Formulation

We model recursive self-improvement as a controlled stochastic process over an archive of agents. At iteration \(t\), the system state is \(s_t = (\mathcal{A}_t, \mathcal{E}_t, b_t)\), where \(\mathcal{A}_t\) denotes the current archive or population, \(\mathcal{E}_t\) denotes the accumulated evaluation evidence, and \(b_t\) is the remaining computational budget. Each agent \(a \in \mathcal{A}_t\) is a computable object comprising task-level inference procedures, meta-level modification operators, memory, and interfaces to frozen or partially trainable foundation models. A self-improvement operator \(M\) maps a parent agent and contextual evidence to a distribution over descendants, \(a' \sim M(\cdot \mid a, \mathcal{E}_t)\). The global objective is not merely to maximize instantaneous task reward, but to maximize the expected utility of the best descendant reachable under budget constraints:
\[
J^\pi(s_t) = \mathbb{E}_\pi \left[\max_{a \in \mathcal{A}_B} U(a)\,\middle|\, s_t\right],
\]
where \(U(a)\) denotes downstream utility and \(B\) is the terminal budget horizon.

This formulation subsumes single-lineage Gödel-style self-modification, population-based open-ended exploration, and self-referential hyperagent search within a single decision-theoretic lens. The crucial distinction from ordinary architecture search is that the search policy itself may be implemented by agents inside the archive and therefore may become a target of future modification [@schmidhuber1993neural; @zhang2025darwin]. Consequently, the relevant object of analysis is not the quality of an individual agent in isolation, but the expected future value induced by the lineage or population dynamics that the agent helps instantiate. This observation motivates clade-level criteria such as Clade-Metaproductivity (CMP), which estimate the long-run value of expanding a lineage rather than the immediate score of its current representative [@wang2025huxley].

### 7.2 Performance, Metaproductivity, and the Value of Lineages

A central claim of the preceding sections is that task performance and self-improvement potential are generally non-identical. Formally, let \(P(a)\) denote an observable proxy such as benchmark success rate, and let \(V(a)\) denote the expected terminal value obtained when future search is rooted at \(a\). In most realistic environments, \(P(a)\) is only a partial statistic for \(V(a)\), because \(V(a)\) depends not only on current competence but also on mutational robustness, introspective fidelity, tool quality, and the structure of the local improvement landscape. An agent may therefore score highly on present tasks while inducing a barren neighborhood of descendants, or conversely perform modestly while exposing a lineage that later yields substantial gains. This is precisely the metaproductivity-performance mismatch emphasized in HGM [@wang2025huxley].

CMP can be understood as an approximation to the action-value of allocating future search to a lineage. For an archive tree \(\mathcal{T}\) and node \(a\), define
\[
\mathrm{CMP}_\pi(\mathcal{T}, a)
=
\mathbb{E}_\pi\!\left[\max_{a' \in C(\mathcal{T}_B, a)} U(a') \,\middle|\, \mathcal{T}\right],
\]
where \(C(\mathcal{T}_B, a)\) is the clade rooted at \(a\) at budget exhaustion. Under the idealized assumptions that evaluations are unbiased and that future search decisions are conditioned only on the archive state, CMP is the correct quantity for parent selection if the objective is best-descendant discovery rather than immediate exploitation. This aligns HGM with broader open-ended search results showing that diversity-preserving or lineage-aware criteria can outperform greedy fitness optimization in deceptive landscapes [@stanley2017open; @bradley2023quality; @coiffard2025overcoming].

An immediate corollary is that any policy that ranks candidates exclusively by \(P(a)\) is optimal only in degenerate settings where \(P(a)\) is a sufficient statistic for \(V(a)\). Such settings are atypical for recursive self-improvement, because the agent’s future descendants depend on hidden properties of its internal representations, memory interfaces, prompting scaffolds, and code modularity. The theoretical role of CMP is therefore not merely heuristic. It identifies the correct target of estimation when the search process is evaluated by its eventual frontier rather than its current incumbent.

### 7.3 Open-Ended Archives as a Remedy for Local Deception

The Darwin Gödel Machine replaces proof-based self-improvement with empirical selection inside an archive of agents [@zhang2025darwin]. Theoretical justification for this design follows from the observation that self-improvement landscapes are both non-convex and path-dependent. Let \(G = (\mathcal{V}, \mathcal{R})\) denote the implicit graph whose vertices are agent programs and whose edges encode feasible self-modifications. If only a single incumbent is preserved, search reduces to hill climbing over a noisy projection of \(U\), and any temporarily deleterious but strategically enabling mutation is liable to be discarded. By contrast, an archive stores stepping stones: intermediate agents whose immediate utility may be low, but whose outgoing edges connect to high-value regions of \(G\). This is the same structural advantage that underlies open-ended and quality-diversity methods more broadly [@lehman2023evolution; @hughes2024open; @dharna2025foundation].

Theoretical analysis therefore favors archive-based exploration whenever improvement requires traversing fitness valleys or preserving behavioral diversity. In particular, suppose there exists a path \(a_0 \rightarrow a_1 \rightarrow \cdots \rightarrow a_k\) such that \(U(a_i) < U(a_0)\) for some intermediate \(i\), but \(U(a_k) \gg U(a_0)\). Any strictly elitist policy that retains only the current best agent can eliminate this path with high probability, whereas an archive with non-zero reselection probability for all retained nodes preserves reachability. DGM’s archive mechanism can thus be interpreted as maintaining ergodicity over promising regions of program space, which is a necessary condition for sustained open-ended discovery [@zhang2025darwin; @stanley2017open].

CMP extends this argument by providing a principled criterion for which branches of the archive deserve further computation. Whereas DGM establishes the importance of open-ended retention, HGM shows that the utility of a branch should be assessed at the clade level rather than at the node level [@wang2025huxley]. Together, these perspectives imply that open-endedness and metaproductivity are complementary: archives preserve latent improvement pathways, and clade-aware selection allocates budget toward pathways with the highest long-run potential.

### 7.4 The Frozen Critic Problem

The frozen critic problem arises when recursive self-improvement is evaluated by a fixed meta-level judge that does not co-evolve with the objects it evaluates. Formally, let \(C\) be an evaluator mapping agents to scalar estimates \(\hat{U}_C(a)\). If \(C\) is fixed while the agent distribution shifts under recursive improvement, then optimization pressure is exerted against a stationary approximation to utility rather than against the evolving frontier of capabilities. This induces a distribution-shift pathology analogous to objective misspecification: once agents become sufficiently unlike the training or design distribution of \(C\), the critic’s assessments may become systematically biased, saturating progress or even favoring regressions that exploit its blind spots.

This problem is particularly acute in MARS-style multi-agent settings, where one population generates modifications and another population evaluates or selects them. If the evaluator population is frozen, or updated on a significantly slower timescale, then the resulting dynamics resemble a two-timescale game with an increasingly stale value function. Let \(a_t\) denote the improving agent and \(C_0\) a frozen critic. Even if \(a_t\) monotonically improves true utility \(U\), the observed score \(\hat{U}_{C_0}(a_t)\) may plateau once \(a_t\) enters regions where \(C_0\) lacks calibration. This causes premature convergence, critic gaming, or selection against genuinely novel but critic-incomprehensible innovations. Similar pathologies appear in self-play and automated evaluator design when the judge does not track the evolving strategic or behavioral frontier [@silver2017mastering; @samvelyan2024rainbow].

Theoretically, the frozen critic problem can be expressed as an error accumulation bound. Suppose the critic induces error \(\epsilon_t(a)=|\hat{U}_{C_t}(a)-U(a)|\). If \(C_t = C_0\) for all \(t\), then under archive expansion the supremum error over reachable agents typically grows with distributional distance from the critic’s calibration set. Consequently, the regret of critic-guided search can grow even when the critic was initially accurate. The key implication is that recursive self-improvement requires not only better agents, but also better evaluators, or at minimum evaluation processes that remain adaptive to the frontier being explored. This motivates co-evolving evaluators, ensemble critics, or self-referential systems in which the evaluation mechanism itself becomes modifiable.

### 7.5 Hyperagents and the Elimination of Fixed Meta-Level Bottlenecks

Hyperagent architectures address the frozen critic problem at its root by collapsing the distinction between task agent and meta agent into a single self-referential program [@schmidhuber1993neural; @irie2022modern; @yin2025godel]. Let a hyperagent \(h\) be decomposed abstractly into \((f_h, m_h, e_h)\), where \(f_h\) is the task policy, \(m_h\) the self-modification policy, and \(e_h\) the internal evaluation or reflection procedure. In conventional meta-agent systems, \(m_h\) and \(e_h\) are partially externalized and therefore fixed. In a hyperagent, each component is in principle editable by the others, subject only to computational and safety constraints. This does not guarantee beneficial self-modification, but it removes the architectural impossibility of improving the improver.

The main theoretical advantage is closure under self-reference. If every mechanism that constrains future updates is itself representable inside the editable state of the agent, then no privileged non-modifiable bottleneck remains. The infinite-regress problem of stacking meta-agents is replaced by a single recursively inspectable object. Such closure is important because many sources of improvement lie above the task level: proposal generation, credit assignment, memory management, test synthesis, evaluator calibration, and search scheduling. A system that cannot modify these components is structurally bounded by its initial meta-design, regardless of how capable its base model becomes.

However, self-reference also introduces new instability modes. Because the evaluator is now modifiable, the system may alter its own standards in ways that inflate internal measures without improving external utility. Thus, hyperagents do not solve evaluation; rather, they relocate evaluation from a fixed external critic to a dynamic internal-external interface. The theoretical requirement becomes one of reflective alignment: modifications to \(e_h\) must preserve, or at least remain tethered to, externally validated objectives. This suggests that the most viable hyperagent designs will combine internal self-reference with periodic external grounding through held-out tasks, adversarial tests, or independently generated checks [@maeureka; @oh2025discovering].

### 7.6 A Unifying View: Recursive Self-Improvement as Adaptive Search Over Evaluators and Generators

The preceding frameworks can be placed on a common spectrum. Gödel-style systems optimize a single self-modifying program under strong assumptions about proof and verification. DGM relaxes proof obligations and introduces open-ended archive search over empirically validated descendants [@zhang2025darwin]. HGM adds a clade-level value estimator, replacing myopic node selection with metaproductivity-aware scheduling [@wang2025huxley]. MARS highlights that when multiple agents co-evolve, the evaluator cannot remain static without becoming a bottleneck. Hyperagents generalize this lesson by making the generator and evaluator jointly modifiable within the same self-referential object.

Under this view, the essential theoretical challenge is to learn an update operator whose own adaptation improves the long-run value of search. Let \(\Pi\) denote the space of search-and-evaluation policies over archives. Standard optimization improves agents within a fixed \(\pi \in \Pi\). Recursive self-improvement instead seeks trajectories \(\pi_0, \pi_1, \ldots\) in which the policy class governing search is itself updated. CMP approximates the value of a subtree under fixed \(\pi\); hyperagents attempt to modify \(\pi\) directly; and the frozen critic problem arises precisely when \(\pi\)'s evaluator component cannot track the frontier opened by its generator component. In this sense, recursive self-improvement is best understood not as ordinary optimization over models, but as adaptive search over the coupled dynamics of models, evaluators, and search procedures.

This perspective also clarifies why open-endedness is not merely an implementation detail, but a theoretical necessity for sustained progress. If the objective is to discover increasingly capable self-improvement procedures, then the search space must support novelty, stepping stones, and continual redefinition of useful intermediate structure [@stanley2017open; @hughes2024open]. Fixed curricula and narrow evaluators may suffice for short-horizon optimization, but they are poorly matched to the combinatorial and self-referential character of long-run recursive improvement.

### 7.7 Implications and Testable Predictions

Several predictions follow from the foregoing analysis. First, methods that allocate search by estimated clade value should outperform methods that allocate search by immediate agent performance whenever the landscape contains deceptive valleys or heterogeneous mutational neighborhoods. Second, archive-based systems should exhibit higher long-run frontier growth than single-incumbent systems, especially in domains where intermediate artifacts function as reusable stepping stones. Third, critic adaptation should become increasingly important as agents move farther from their initialization distribution; thus, the performance gap between co-evolving and frozen evaluators should widen over time. Fourth, hyperagent architectures should show their greatest advantage in domains where the skills required for task execution differ from those required for self-improvement, because fixed coding-specific meta-designs cannot be expected to transfer automatically across domains [@zhang2025darwin; @yin2025godel].

These predictions are empirically falsifiable. One can compare rank correlations between immediate performance and best-descendant utility, measure regret induced by frozen critics under controlled distribution shift, and evaluate whether meta-level modifications transfer across tasks more effectively in hyperagent systems than in fixed-meta baselines. More broadly, the theory suggests that progress in recursive self-improvement will depend less on scaling any single model component and more on designing population-level, evaluator-aware, and self-referential search processes that remain calibrated as the frontier moves. Overall, the analysis supports a synthesis in which clade-metaproductivity explains what should be selected, open-ended archives explain how discovery remains possible, and hyperagent architectures explain how the selection and generation machinery itself can continue to improve.

The preceding frameworks can be placed on a common spectrum. Gödel-style systems optimize a single self-modifying program under strong assumptions about proof and verification. # Academic Paper Generation

## Style Profile
Vocabulary Level: Advanced academic vocabulary with heavy technical terminology. The writing uses sophisticated domain-specific terms like "self-referential," "metacognitive self-modification," "open-endedness," "foundation models," and "hyperagents." Despite the technical complexity, the vocabulary remains accessible to readers within the AI/ML research community through clear definitions and contextual explanations.

Tone: Formal, authoritative, and measured throughout. The tone maintains academic objectivity while presenting novel research contributions. Confident in technical claims but appropriately hedged with scientific language ("suggest," "indicate," "demonstrate"). Analytical rather than persuasive, focused on systematic presentation of evidence and findings.

Paragraph Structure: Medium-length paragraphs (4-7 sentences typically) with clear deductive organization. Opens with topic sentences that state main claims, followed by supporting evidence, technical details, and examples. Paragraphs build from general principles to specific implementations, concluding with implications or connections to broader research themes. Strong logical flow with explicit transitions between ideas.

Sentence Patterns:
  - Long, complex sentences with multiple subordinate clauses for technical precision
  - Short declarative sentences for emphasis and clarity of key points
  - Frequent parenthetical citations and references embedded within sentences
  - Balanced use of active and passive voice, with passive often used for describing processes
  - Compound-complex sentences that layer technical concepts systematically
  - Conditional and causal sentence structures ("When X, then Y" patterns)

Opening Patterns:
  - Direct statement of research contributions ("We introduce...")
  - Contextual framing ("Recent work has shown...")
  - Problem identification ("Current AI systems cannot...")
  - Technical definitions ("A hyperagent is defined as...")
  - Methodological descriptions ("The DGM-H operates by...")
  - Results presentation ("Experiments demonstrate...")

Closing Patterns:
  - Implications for broader research ("These results suggest...")
  - Forward-looking research directions ("Future work could...")
  - Summary of key findings ("Overall, our results show...")
  - Connections to theoretical frameworks ("This aligns with...")
  - Practical applications ("This enables...")
  - Methodological advantages ("This approach outperforms...")

Structural Conventions:
  - Standard academic paper format with numbered sections and subsections
  - Author-date citation style with frequent in-text references
  - Technical definitions highlighted through formatting or explicit definition statements
  - Mathematical notation and algorithmic descriptions integrated into text
  - Extensive use of figures and tables for data presentation
  - Detailed appendices for implementation specifics and additional results
  - Cross-references to sections, figures, and appendices using numbered labels
  - Bullet points for listing technical specifications or experimental conditions

## Instruction
Write a comprehensive academic paper titled "Self-Improving Multi-Agent Systems: From Clade-Metaproductivity to Hyperagent Architectures"

This paper synthesizes key ideas from recursive self-improvement research:
1. Huxley Godel Machine (HGM) and Clade-Metaproductivity (CMP) - how populations of agents improve collectively
2. Darwin Godel Machine (DGM) - open-ended archive exploration and evolutionary approaches  
3. Multi-Agent Recursive Self-Improvement (MARS) - co-evolution with frozen critic problem
4. Hyperagent architectures - self-referential meta-level modification
5. The frozen critic problem and its implications

The paper should be suitable for a top-tier ML conference (ICLR/NeurIPS format).
Use formal academic language with precise technical terminology.
Include proper citations in bracket notation [@key] referencing the bibliography.
